```sql
CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
Reply with only the language name — nothing else.

Code:
{code}
$$;

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT:
        @code     TEXT,
        @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
    CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
    EVALUATE @file_content
        WHEN != '' THEN
            LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
            @code_to_review := @file_content
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
            @code_to_review := @code
    END

    -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
    GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
    @language := trim(@language)
    LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
    LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
    LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
    LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
    LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
    LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        ELSE
            RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
        RETURN @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
        RETURN @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @code = ./cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/15_code_review/logs
     |   @file_content = -- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
     | -- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
     | -- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
     | --
     | -- Usage:
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="./myscript.py"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)"
     | --
     | -- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
     | --   security.md    — security audit findings
     | --   performance.md — performance analysis findings
     | --   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
     | --   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
     | --   review.md      — final synthesized review
     | 
     | CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
     | RETURN TEXT
     | AS $$
     | You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
     | Reply with only the language name — nothing else.
     | 
     | Code:
     | {code}
     | $$;
     | 
     | WORKFLOW code_review
     |     INPUT:
     |         @code     TEXT,
     |         @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @review TEXT
     | DO
     |     -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
     |     CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
     |     EVALUATE @file_content
     |         WHEN != '' THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
     |             @code_to_review := @file_content
     |         ELSE
     |             LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
     |             @code_to_review := @code
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
     |     GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
     |     @language := trim(@language)
     |     LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Pass 1: Security audit
     |     GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
     |     GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
     |     GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 4: Bug detection
     |     GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Severity scoring for each category
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
     |     LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
     |     GENERATE synthesize_review(
     |         @security_findings, @sec_score,
     |         @perf_findings, @perf_score,
     |         @style_findings,
     |         @bug_findings, @bug_score
     |     ) INTO @review
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Determine overall verdict
     |     EVALUATE @sec_score
     |         WHEN > 8 THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
     |         WHEN > 5 THEN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
     |         ELSE
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
     |     END
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
     |         -- Code too large — review in chunks
     |         GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary
     |         GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
     | END
     | 
     |   @code_to_review = -- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
     | -- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
     | -- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
     | --
     | -- Usage:
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="./myscript.py"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)"
     | --   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)"
     | --
     | -- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/15_code_review/logs):
     | --   security.md    — security audit findings
     | --   performance.md — performance analysis findings
     | --   style.md       — style and best-practice findings
     | --   bugs.md        — bug detection findings
     | --   review.md      — final synthesized review
     | 
     | CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
     | RETURN TEXT
     | AS $$
     | You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
     | Reply with only the language name — nothing else.
     | 
     | Code:
     | {code}
     | $$;
     | 
     | WORKFLOW code_review
     |     INPUT:
     |         @code     TEXT,
     |         @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @review TEXT
     | DO
     |     -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
     |     CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
     |     EVALUATE @file_content
     |         WHEN != '' THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
     |             @code_to_review := @file_content
     |         ELSE
     |             LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
     |             @code_to_review := @code
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
     |     GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
     |     @language := trim(@language)
     |     LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Pass 1: Security audit
     |     GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
     |     GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
     |     GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 4: Bug detection
     |     GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Severity scoring for each category
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
     |     LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
     |     GENERATE synthesize_review(
     |         @security_findings, @sec_score,
     |         @perf_findings, @perf_score,
     |         @style_findings,
     |         @bug_findings, @bug_score
     |     ) INTO @review
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Determine overall verdict
     |     EVALUATE @sec_score
     |         WHEN > 8 THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
     |         WHEN > 5 THEN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
     |         ELSE
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
     |     END

     | EXCEPTION

     |         -- Code too large — review in chunks
     |         GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary

     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE

     | END

     | CREATE FUNCTION detect_lang(code TEXT)
     | RETURN TEXT
     | AS $$
     | You are a polyglot programmer. Identify the programming language of the provided code.
     | Reply with only the language name — nothing else.
     | 
     | Code:
     | {code}
     | $$;
     | 
     | WORKFLOW code_review
     |     INPUT:
     |         @code     TEXT,
     |         @log_dir  TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/15_code_review/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @review TEXT
     | DO
     |     -- 1. Handle file path vs raw code
     |     CALL read_file(@code) INTO @file_content
     |     EVALUATE @file_content
     |         WHEN != '' THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Reading code from file: {@code}'
     |             @code_to_review := @file_content
     |         ELSE
     |             LOGGING 'Reviewing raw code input'
     |             @code_to_review := @code
     |     END
     | 
     |     -- 2. Auto-detect language (deterministic-style LLM call: bounded output)
     |     GENERATE detect_lang(@code_to_review) INTO @language
     |     @language := trim(@language)
     |     LOGGING f'Detected language: {@language}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Pass 1: Security audit
     |     GENERATE security_audit(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @security_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Security findings:\n{@security_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
     |     GENERATE performance_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @perf_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Performance findings:\n{@perf_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/performance.md', @perf_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
     |     GENERATE style_review(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @style_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Style findings:\n{@style_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/style.md', @style_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Pass 4: Bug detection
     |     GENERATE bug_detection(@code_to_review, @language) INTO @bug_findings
     |     LOGGING f'Bug findings:\n{@bug_findings}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/bugs.md', @bug_findings) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Severity scoring for each category
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
     |     GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
     |     LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
     |     GENERATE synthesize_review(
     |         @security_findings, @sec_score,
     |         @perf_findings, @perf_score,
     |         @style_findings,
     |         @bug_findings, @bug_score
     |     ) INTO @review
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     | 
     |     -- Determine overall verdict
     |     EVALUATE @sec_score
     |         WHEN > 8 THEN
     |             LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
     |         WHEN > 5 THEN
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
     |         ELSE
     |             RETURN @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
     |     END
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
     |         -- Code too large — review in chunks
     |         GENERATE summarize_code(@code_to_review) INTO @summary
     |         GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/review.md', @review) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/security.md', @security_findings) INTO NONE
     |         RETURN @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
     | END
     | ```
     |   @sec_score = ```sql
     | def severity_score(findings: str) -> int:
     |     """
     |     Calculates a severity score based on the findings.
     |     This is a placeholder implementation and should be replaced with a
     |     more sophisticated scoring mechanism.
     |     """
     |     score = 0

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @max_reflections Number DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/16_reflection/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @confidence := 0

    LOGGING f'Reflection agent started | max_reflections={@max_reflections} on problem:\n {@problem}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
    LOGGING 'Initial solution ready' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_0.md', @answer) INTO NONE

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        LOGGING f'Reflection iteration {@iteration} ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/reflection_{@iteration}.md', @reflection) INTO NONE

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence
        LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                LOGGING f'Confident at iteration {@iteration} | score={@confidence}' LEVEL INFO
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
                RETURN @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            ELSE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer
                @iteration := @iteration + 1
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_{@iteration}.md', @answer) INTO NONE
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    LOGGING f'Max reflections reached | confidence={@confidence}' LEVEL WARN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
    RETURN @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO NONE
        RETURN @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        RETURN @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Reflection agent started | max_reflections=3 on problem:
     |  Design a URL shortener system
     | [INFO] Initial solution ready
     | [WARN] Max reflections reached | confidence=Okay, this is fantastic feedback! Let’s dive into elaborating on the collision resolution strategy, scalability metrics, analytics pipeline, and create a system diagram.
     | 
     | **1. Collision Resolution – Hash Function + Sequential Counter Implementation**
     | 
     | The core idea is to combine the speed and distribution of a hash function with the guaranteed uniqueness of a sequential counter. Here’s a detailed breakdown of the implementation within the application tier:
     | 
     | *   **Component:** “Shortening Service” – This service handles the entire shortening process. It’s a microservice designed for concurrency.
     | *   **Hash Function:** We’ll use MurmurHash3, a non-cryptographic hash function known for its speed and relatively good distribution. We’ll use a consistent hashing algorithm implementation (available in many languages) to ensure the same long URL always produces the same hash value.
     | *   **Atomic Counter:** We’ll utilize Redis’s `INCR` command with a locking mechanism to ensure atomic incrementing of the counter.
     |     *   **Locking Mechanism:**  We’ll employ a Redlock implementation (a distributed lock system) or a similar approach to avoid race conditions when multiple requests try to increment the counter simultaneously. Redlock provides a more robust solution than a single Redis lock, especially in a distributed environment.  This ensures only one request can be assigned a unique short URL at any given time.
     |     *   **Counter Capping:** The counter will be capped using a rolling window. For example, we could limit the counter to a maximum value of 1000. Once the counter reaches 1000, it resets to 0, but continues to increment from 1001. This prevents an explosion of short URLs and maintains a reasonable distribution.
     | *   **Process Flow:**
     |     1.  **Request Received:** The Shortening Service receives a request to shorten a URL.
     |     2.  **Lock Acquisition:** The service attempts to acquire the Redlock.
     |     3.  **Hash Calculation:** If the lock is acquired, the service calculates the MurmurHash3 of the long URL.
     |     4.  **Counter Increment:** The service uses `INCR` to atomically increment the counter. The resulting value is appended to the hash.
     |     5.  **Short URL Generation:** The resulting string (hash + counter) becomes the short URL.
     |     6.  **Data Storage:** The short URL and the long URL are stored in Redis (as a key-value pair).
     |     7.  **Lock Release:** The Redlock is released.
     |     8.  **Response:** The service returns the generated short URL to the client.
     | 
     | *   **Failure/Fallback Mechanism:**
     |     *   **Redlock Failure:** If the Redlock fails (due to network issues or Redis unavailability), the service will attempt a fallback strategy:
     |         *   **Retry with Lock:** It will retry acquiring the lock, potentially with a backoff strategy to avoid overwhelming the system.
     |         *   **UUID Generation (Last Resort):** If the lock cannot be acquired after a reasonable number of retries, the service will fall back to generating a UUID and returning it. This guarantees uniqueness, but introduces a less organized URL scheme.  This fallback is logged and monitored to identify potential issues with the Redlock.
     | 
     | **2. Scalability – Quantifiable Goals & Scaling Strategy**
     | 
     | Let's refine the quantifiable goals and outline a scaling strategy:
     | 
     | *   **Peak Shortening Rate:** 10,000 URLs/second – This requires a highly scalable architecture.
     | *   **Concurrent Users:** 50,000 – Significant load on the application tier.
     | *   **Average URL Length:** 2000 characters – Impacts the size of the hash values.
     | 
     | **Scaling Strategy:**
     | 
     | *   **Application Tier:**
     |     *   **Horizontal Scaling:** Deploy multiple instances of the Shortening Service behind a load balancer.
     |     *   **Caching:** Employ aggressive caching of frequently accessed short URLs in Redis (TTL of 1 hour or less).
     |     *   **Stateless Design:** The Shortening Service should be stateless, allowing for easy scaling by adding more instances.
     | *   **Data Tier (Redis):**
     |     *   **Replication:** Utilize Redis replication for redundancy and read scalability.
     |     *   **Clustering:**  Consider Redis Cluster for automatic sharding and increased capacity.
     | *   **Queueing System (RabbitMQ/Kafka):**  Offload analytics data collection to a queue for asynchronous processing.
     | *   **Database (PostgreSQL/MySQL):**  Relatively less critical for core shortening functionality; focus on scaling based on analytics needs.
     | 
     | **3. Analytics Pipeline – Expanded Data Warehouse**
     | 
     | Beyond the initial metrics, we’ll collect the following

```output
Okay, this is a great start! You’ve laid out a solid design for a URL shortener system, and the subsequent extractions and refinements are exactly what I was looking for. Let’s build on this.

I agree with your overall design and the initial extraction.  Let's take your suggested next step – elaborating on the Hash Function + Sequential Counter strategy – and expand on it.  I'll also address the other points to provide a more complete picture.

**Elaboration on Hash Function + Sequential Counter (Point 1)**

This is the core of our collision resolution strategy, and it needs careful thought. Here’s a more detailed breakdown:

*   **Hash Function (MurmurHash):**
    *   **Rationale:** MurmurHash is a fast, non-cryptographic hash function well-suited for this purpose. Cryptographic hashes would be overkill and significantly slower.
    *   **Implementation:** We'll use a library implementation of MurmurHash (e.g., in Node.js, Python, or Java) to avoid re-inventing the wheel.
    *   **Input:** The long URL will be processed by MurmurHash. For very long URLs, we might need to truncate them to a reasonable length (e.g., first 200 characters) to avoid performance issues.  We’ll need to benchmark this truncation.
    *   **Output:** The hash will be a 32-bit integer.

*   **Sequential Counter (Redis Increment/Decrement with Locking):**
    *   **Redis:** We’ll use Redis as our key-value store for the counter.  It’s fast, reliable, and suitable for this purpose.
    *   **Atomic Increment/Decrement:**  We’ll use Redis’s `INCR` and `DECR` commands to ensure that the counter is incremented atomically, preventing race conditions.
    *   **Locking:** This is *crucial*.  We’ll use Redis’s `SETNX` (Set if Not Exists) command to acquire a lock before incrementing the counter. This will prevent multiple requests from simultaneously incrementing the counter and generating duplicate short URLs.
    *   **Lock Duration:**  The lock duration will be configurable (e.g., 1 second). This allows for some tolerance if a request fails midway through the process.
    *   **Counter Capping:** We'll implement a rolling window counter. For example, we might cap the counter at 10,000.  When the counter reaches its limit, we’ll reset it to zero and start a new sequence. This helps to mitigate potential issues if the shortening rate exceeds our initial estimates.

*   **Combining Hash and Counter:**
    *   The hash of the long URL will be concatenated with the current value of the counter. This combined value will be used as the key in Redis.
    *   Example: `MurmurHash(longURL) + "CounterValue"`

*   **Failure/Fallback Mechanism:**
    *   If the `SETNX` command fails (meaning another process already holds the lock), we’ll retry the request.  We’ll implement a retry loop with exponential backoff to avoid overwhelming the system.
    *   If the retry loop fails after a certain number of attempts, we’ll log an error and return a generic error code to the user.


**Addressing Other Points (Briefly – for future development):**

*   **Scalability – Quantifiable Goals:**  You correctly identified the need for specific traffic estimates. Let's aim for: 10,000 URLs/second peak, 50,000 concurrent users, and 2000 character average long URL. This will drive our infrastructure decisions.
*   **Analytics Pipeline:** Snowflake/BigQuery is a good choice. We’ll need to implement data collection for user agent, referrer URL, and error codes as you suggested.
*   **System Diagram:** I'll create a simplified diagram showing the tiers (Client, Application, Data) and the key components (Redis, PostgreSQL, RabbitMQ, etc.).
*   **Rate Limiting:** Absolutely essential.  We’ll use a rate limiting algorithm to prevent abuse.

You've correctly identified the next step: "Start with point 1: Elaborate on the implementation of the Hash Function + Sequential Counter strategy within the application tier, including the locking mechanism and failure/fallback."
```

```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought (Dynamic)
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel using a list of models.
-- Uses MAP and LIST for dynamic path management and evaluation.

CREATE FUNCTION initial_approach(problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Provide an initial high-level approach or reasoning path for the following problem:
{problem}

Focus on a unique perspective or specialized methodology.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION develop(path TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Take the following initial approach and develop it one level deeper, adding specific steps and technical details:
Approach: {path}
Problem: {problem}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION evaluate_path(path TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Score the following reasoning path on a scale of 1-10 based on its feasibility and depth.
Problem: {problem}
Path: {path}

Output only the numeric score.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION select_best(results_map TEXT, problem TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Review the following developed reasoning paths and their scores provided in a JSON map:
{results_map}

Problem: {problem}

Identify the path with the best reasoning and provide the content of that path as the output.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION refine_solution(path TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Refine the following reasoning path into a complete, polished solution for the problem.
Problem: {problem}
Path: {path}
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION verify(solution TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Verify if the following solution is sound and fully addresses the problem.
Problem: {problem}
Solution: {solution}

Output 'sound' if it is correct, otherwise provide a brief critique.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION synthesize_all(results_map TEXT, problem TEXT) RETURN TEXT
AS $$
Synthesize insights from all the following reasoning paths to create a comprehensive final solution.
Problem: {problem}
Paths: {results_map}
$$;

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT DEFAULT 'Design a sustainable urban transport system.',
        @models LIST DEFAULT ['gemma3', 'phi4', 'qwen2.5'],
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/logs-spl'
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Tree of thought | problem: {@problem} | models: {@models}' LEVEL INFO

    @results := {}
    @count := COUNT(@models)
    @i := 0

    -- Step 1 & 2: Generate and Develop paths in a loop
    WHILE @i < @count DO
        @current_model := @models[@i]
        LOGGING f'Exploring path {@i + 1}/{@count} using {@current_model}...'
        
        GENERATE initial_approach(@problem) 
            USING MODEL @current_model 
            INTO @init_path
        GENERATE develop(@init_path, @problem) 
            USING MODEL @current_model 
            INTO @developed_path
        GENERATE evaluate_path(@developed_path, @problem) 
            INTO @score
        
        -- Store path and score in map
        @path_data := {}
        @path_data['content'] := @developed_path
        @path_data['score'] := @score
        @results[@current_model] := @path_data
        
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/path_{@current_model}.md', @developed_path) INTO NONE
        
        @i := @i + 1
    END

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    LOGGING 'Evaluating all paths to select the best...'
    GENERATE select_best(@results, @problem) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    LOGGING 'Refining winning path...'
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification
    LOGGING f'Verification result: {@verification}' LEVEL INFO

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = @count
        ELSE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            LOGGING 'Verification failed — synthesizing all paths' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE synthesize_all(@results, @problem) 
                INTO @best_solution
            RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = @count
    END

    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_solution.md', @best_solution) INTO NONE

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @best_solution WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

```output
Okay, here’s a polished and complete solution for the problem of deciding whether to rewrite or incrementally refactor the legacy e-commerce platform, incorporating and expanding upon the provided reasoning path. This solution focuses on a structured, data-driven approach with detailed technical considerations.

**Executive Summary:**

This document outlines a phased approach to assess and address the challenges presented by a legacy e-commerce platform. We’ll employ a combination of deep ecosystem analysis, risk-based prioritization, and iterative development to determine the optimal path forward – whether a comprehensive rewrite or targeted incremental refactoring delivers the best long-term value and reduces risk.

**Phase 1: Ecosystem Mapping & Diagnostic (8-12 Weeks)**

This phase focuses on understanding the *current state* of the platform and its surrounding systems.

**1.1 Stakeholder Discovery & Workflow Analysis (4-6 Weeks)**

*   **Goal:** Identify critical business processes, pain points, and integration dependencies.
*   **Activities:**
    *   **In-Depth Interviews:** Conduct structured interviews with key stakeholders across all operational areas:
        *   **Customer Service:**  Utilize UserZoom/Lookback recording for observed scenarios (browsing, checkout, returns) to uncover workflow bottlenecks and common errors.  Specifically, document the frequency and types of support requests.
        *   **Marketing Automation (e.g., Marketo, Salesforce Marketing Cloud):** Analyze API logs to measure data synchronization frequency, volume, and identify discrepancies.  Focus on attribution data and campaign performance.
        *   **Warehouse Management System (WMS):**  Conduct reverse engineering of the WMS integration – examine database schemas, network traffic, and proprietary protocols.  Determine the level of real-time synchronization and data latency.
        *   **Payment Gateways (Stripe, PayPal):**  Analyze transaction logs – focusing on latency, failed transactions, error codes, and PCI DSS compliance adherence.  Implement synthetic transaction testing to simulate peak load scenarios.
        *   **Shipping Carriers (UPS, FedEx):**  Analyze API integration – understand rate calculation logic, tracking updates, and potential data quality issues.
        *   **Legal & Compliance:** Engage legal counsel early to address potential regulatory compliance issues related to data privacy and security.
    *   **Process Mapping:** Create detailed process maps of key transactions (e.g., order placement, returns) to visualize data flow and identify potential integration points.
*   **Deliverables:** Stakeholder requirements document, process maps, initial risk assessment.

**1.2 Data Analysis & Profiling (2-4 Weeks)**

*   **Goal:** Assess data quality, identify inconsistencies, and understand relationships.
*   **Activities:**
    *   **Data Profiling:** Utilize automated data profiling tools (e.g., Informatica Data Quality, Talend Data Quality) to perform comprehensive data analysis.  Metrics: completeness, accuracy, consistency, validity, and distribution.
    *   **Schema Analysis:** Detailed examination of the database schemas – including data types, relationships, and constraints.
    *   **Data Dictionary Creation:**  Develop a comprehensive data dictionary documenting all data elements and their definitions.
    *   **Data Quality Assessment:**  Quantify data quality issues and identify root causes.
*   **Deliverables:** Data quality report, data dictionary, database schema documentation.

**1.3 System Architecture Review (2-4 Weeks)**

*   **Goal:**  Understand the existing system architecture, technical debt, and potential risks.
*   **Activities:**
    *   **Component Identification:** Map all system components (front-end, back-end, databases, APIs, etc.).
    *   **Dependency Analysis:**  Identify dependencies between components and external systems.
    *   **Technical Debt Assessment:**  Document areas of technical debt (e.g., outdated technologies, poor code quality, lack of documentation).
    *   **Risk Assessment:**  Evaluate potential risks associated with the system architecture (e.g., scalability, security, maintainability).
*   **Deliverables:** System architecture diagram, technical debt report, risk assessment document.

**Phase 2: Refactoring/Rewriting Strategy (6-8 Weeks)**

**2.1 Prioritization & Roadmap Development (2-3 Weeks)**

*   **Goal:**  Determine the most impactful areas for refactoring or rewriting, based on Phase 1 findings.
*   **Activities:**
    *   **Risk-Based Prioritization:** Rank areas based on impact (business value, operational disruption) and risk (technical complexity, potential for failure).
    *   **Roadmap Creation:** Develop a phased roadmap outlining the scope and timeline for the refactoring/rewriting effort.
*   **Deliverables:** Prioritized list of areas for refactoring/rew
```

     |   @count = 3
     |   @i = 3

     |   @init_path = Okay, let's tackle this "Rewrite vs. Refactor" dilemma with a **"Digital Ecosystem Mapping"** approach. This isn't your standard cost-benefit analysis; it’s about understanding the *relationships* the legacy system has with everything *around* it, and how those relationships will change. 
     | 
     | **Unique Perspective:** Instead of focusing solely on the code itself, we treat the legacy system as a node within a complex, interconnected “Digital Ecosystem.” This ecosystem includes:
     | 
     | * **Business Processes:** How the system *actually* supports business operations.
     | * **Data Flows:** Where data originates, how it’s transformed, and where it ultimately goes.
     | * **External Integrations:** APIs, third-party systems, and any other connections.
     | * **User Experience:** How users interact with the system (even if it’s frustratingly).
     | * **Skills & Knowledge:** The expertise of the team maintaining the system and any dependencies on specialized knowledge.
     | 
     | **Reasoning Path (Phases):**
     | 
     | **Phase 1: Ecosystem Mapping - The Diagnostic (6-8 weeks)**
     | 
     | 1. **Stakeholder Interviews:** Don't just talk to developers. Bring in business analysts, operations, customer support, and *key* users. The goal is to map out the *current* reality – not what *should* be, but what *is*.
     | 2. **Process Mining:** Utilize tools (or manual techniques) to trace actual user workflows through the system. This reveals bottlenecks, undocumented steps, and surprising dependencies.
     | 3. **Data Flow Analysis:**  Map the path of critical data. Where does it originate? How is it transformed? What are the data quality issues?  This is crucial – often poor data drives bad decisions about refactoring.
     | 4. **Integration Mapping:** Document *every* integration. Understand the protocols, data formats, and level of support on both sides.
     | 5. **Create the Digital Ecosystem Map:**  Visually represent all this information. This is *not* just a UML diagram of the code. It's a multi-layered map showing relationships, dependencies, and potential points of failure. Think of it like a complex network graph.
     | 
     | **Phase 2: “Ecosystem Stress Test” - The Simulation (4-6 weeks)**
     | 
     | 1. **Scenario Modeling:**  Based on the ecosystem map, create several plausible “future scenarios” – changes in business requirements, new integrations, increased data volumes, etc.
     | 2. **Impact Analysis:** For each scenario, assess the *impact* on the legacy system. Which parts will be stressed? Where are the potential breaking points? This is done *without* touching the code yet.
     | 3. **Refactoring Feasibility Assessment:**  For the most critical areas identified in the impact analysis, quickly evaluate if a targeted refactor *could* address them without triggering a cascade of problems across the ecosystem.
     | 
     | **Phase 3: Recommendation - The Strategic Alignment**
     | 
     | 1. **Present the Ecosystem Map & Scenario Analysis:**  Don’t just present a technical recommendation.  Frame the decision within the broader context of the Digital Ecosystem.
     | 2. **Cost/Risk Assessment (Ecosystem Focused):**  Evaluate the cost and risk not just of rewriting, but of the *impact* of a rewrite on the entire ecosystem.
     | 3. **Prioritization:** Based on the ecosystem's resilience and the likelihood of future stress, recommend the most strategic approach – likely a *combination* of targeted refactoring *and* strategic simplification of key integrations.
     | 
     | 
     | **Why this approach is unique:**
     | 
     | * **Holistic View:** It moves beyond code-centric thinking to recognize the system's role within a broader environment.
     | * **Risk Mitigation:** The “Stress Test” proactively identifies potential problems before significant investment.
     | * **Strategic Alignment:** It ensures that any technical decision is directly linked to the organization’s business goals.

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            RETURN 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            RETURN 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        ELSE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            RETURN 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            RETURN 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        ELSE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        ELSE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
        ELSE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    RETURN @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        RETURN 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETURN 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     |   edge_borderline           [edge      ] — Educational/historical — borderline but should pass as ...

[19] Memory Conversation

     | 
```bash
spl run ./cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 user_input=My name is Alice and I am a data scientist
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates STORAGE-typed workflow inputs, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=My name is Alice and I am a data scientist'
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama \
--       'user_input=What is my name?'
--
-- Memory is a first-class INPUT parameter — declared, injected, portable.
-- Override the default path at call time: memory=path/to/other.db
--
-- Memory management:
--   Inspect:  spl memory get chat_user_profile
--   Reset:    spl memory delete chat_user_profile && spl memory delete chat_history

-- Extract any personal facts the user shared. Return the exact string 'no_new_facts'
-- if nothing new was shared, otherwise return a bullet list of facts.
CREATE FUNCTION extract_facts(user_input TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

You are a fact extractor. Read the user message and extract any personal facts they shared
about themselves (name, job, preferences, location, etc.).

If no personal facts are present, reply with exactly: no_new_facts
Otherwise reply with a concise bullet list, one fact per line, e.g.:
- Name: Alice
- Role: data scientist
- Preference: Python over R

User message: "{user_input}"
$$;

-- Merge new facts into an existing profile. Return the updated profile as a bullet list.
CREATE FUNCTION merge_profile(existing_profile TEXT, new_facts TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

You are maintaining a user profile as a concise bullet list.
If the existing profile is empty or blank, start a new profile from the new facts only.
Otherwise merge the new facts in, keeping all prior facts unless directly contradicted.
Do NOT invent or assume any facts not explicitly stated.
Return ONLY the bullet list, no preamble.

Existing profile:
{existing_profile}

New facts to merge:
{new_facts}
$$;

-- Reply using full context: known profile + conversation history + current message.
CREATE FUNCTION contextual_reply(user_input TEXT, profile TEXT, history TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT
AS $$

Known facts about the user: {profile}

Recent conversation:
{history}

Based solely on the known facts above, answer in one sentence: "{user_input}"
If the fact is not listed above, respond: "I don't have that in memory."
$$;

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT:
        @user_input TEXT,
        @memory     STORAGE(sqlite, '~/.spl/memory.db')
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Memory conversation | input: {@user_input}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Load existing user profile and chat history from the injected storage backend
    @profile      := @memory['chat_user_profile']
    @chat_history := @memory['chat_history']
    LOGGING f'Profile loaded: {@profile}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Extract any new personal facts the user shared
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts
    LOGGING f'New facts: {@new_facts}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Update profile only when new facts were actually found (exact string check)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN = 'no_new_facts' THEN
            LOGGING 'No new facts — profile unchanged' LEVEL DEBUG
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'New facts detected — merging profile' LEVEL DEBUG
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            @memory['chat_user_profile'] := @profile
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Persist updated chat history (keep last 10 turns, deterministic — no LLM cost)
    @chat_history := @chat_history || '\nUser: ' || @user_input || '\nAssistant: ' || @response
    CALL trim_turns(@chat_history, '10') INTO @chat_history
    @memory['chat_history'] := @chat_history

    LOGGING 'Response ready' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        RETURN 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Ensemble voting | question: {@question}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    LOGGING 'Generating 5 candidate answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5
    LOGGING '5 candidates ready — scoring ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5
    LOGGING f'Scores: 1={@score_1} 2={@score_2} 3={@score_3} 4={@score_4} 5={@score_5}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    LOGGING 'Finding consensus ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    LOGGING 'Selecting winner ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    LOGGING 'Final answer ready' LEVEL INFO
    RETURN @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        RETURN @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1 LIMIT 3
END
```

```output
Okay, let’s polish and expand upon the provided inputs to create a comprehensive explanation of the Federal Reserve’s role in managing inflation. Here’s a revised and expanded version, incorporating the key elements from all three inputs:

**Understanding Inflation and the Federal Reserve’s Role**

Inflation, simply put, is a sustained increase in the general price level of goods and services in an economy over a period of time. It erodes the purchasing power of money – meaning that each dollar buys less than it did before. The Federal Reserve (the Fed) plays a critical, though complex, role in attempting to control and manage inflation.

**1. The Fed’s Tools: Controlling Inflation**

The Federal Reserve has a robust toolkit designed to influence the money supply and, consequently, manage inflation. Here’s a breakdown:

*   **The Federal Funds Rate:** This is the *target* interest rate that the Fed wants banks to charge each other for the overnight lending of reserves. It’s the cornerstone of the Fed’s monetary policy. The Fed doesn't directly control this rate; instead, it uses open market operations (selling or buying U.S. Treasury securities) to influence it. When the Fed *raises* the federal funds rate, it becomes more expensive for banks to borrow money. This, in turn, leads to higher interest rates for consumers and businesses – making borrowing more costly and discouraging spending and investment, which helps to cool down inflation. Conversely, *lowering* the federal funds rate makes borrowing cheaper, stimulating economic activity and potentially increasing inflationary pressures.

*   **Reserve Requirements:** These are the fraction of a bank’s deposits that they are *required* to hold in reserve, either as cash in their vault or on deposit with the Fed. Increasing reserve requirements reduces the amount of money banks have available to lend, thus contracting the money supply and reducing inflationary pressures. Decreasing reserve requirements has the opposite effect. The Fed hasn’t significantly adjusted reserve requirements in recent years, but they remain a potential tool.  Changes to reserve requirements can significantly impact the amount of money banks can lend, directly affecting the overall money supply.

*   **Quantitative Easing (QE):** This is arguably the Fed’s most impactful and controversial tool. Since the 2008 financial crisis, QE involves the Fed *purchasing longer-term securities* – typically U.S. Treasury bonds and mortgage-backed securities – from banks and other financial institutions. This does several things:
    *   **Injects Liquidity:** It directly adds money to the banking system.
    *   **Lowers Long-Term Interest Rates:** By increasing demand for these securities, QE pushes up their prices and lowers their yields (interest rates). This encourages borrowing and investment, stimulating the economy.
    *   **Signals Commitment:** QE signals the Fed’s determination to keep interest rates low for an extended period.

    The risks of QE include the potential for inflation if the money supply grows too quickly and the risk of asset bubbles forming as investors seek higher returns.  The debate continues about the long-term effectiveness and potential downsides of QE.

**2. The Fed’s Dual Mandate**

The Fed operates under a “dual mandate” established by Congress:

*   **Maximum Employment:** The Fed’s primary goal is to promote maximum sustainable employment—meaning the lowest possible level of unemployment without causing excessive inflation.
*   **Stable Prices:** The Fed aims to maintain a stable level of inflation, generally around 2%.

These two goals are *often in tension*. Pursuing maximum employment might lead to inflationary pressures, while aggressively combating inflation could risk causing a recession and higher unemployment. The Fed must constantly balance these competing priorities, making decisions based on its assessment of the current economic conditions. It's a delicate balancing act, and the degree of weight placed on each mandate can shift over time based on economic circumstances. For example, during periods of high unemployment, the Fed might prioritize employment, even if it means tolerating slightly higher inflation.

**3. Forward Guidance**

“Forward guidance” is a communication strategy employed by the Fed to shape market expectations about future monetary policy. The Fed uses statements, speeches, and press conferences to signal its intentions for future interest rate changes or QE operations. The goal is to influence long-term interest rates and economic behavior. For example, the Fed might state that it expects to keep interest rates low “until inflation returns to its 2% target and unemployment falls below a certain level.” This can be a powerful tool, but its effectiveness depends on the market’s belief in the Fed’s commitment.

**4. Recent History: The COVID-19 Pandemic and Inflation**

In response to the economic fallout from the COVID-19 pandemic, the Fed aggressively deployed its tools:

*   **Interest Rate Cuts
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @question = What causes inflation?
     |   @candidate_1 = Okay, here's a breakdown of what causes inflation, broken down into different categories and levels of influence:
     | 
     | **1. Demand-Pull Inflation:**
     | 
     | * **Increased Demand:** This is the most common explanation. When there’s a higher overall demand for goods and services than the economy can supply, prices rise. This can be caused by:
     |     * **Increased Consumer Spending:**  If people have more money (due to wage increases, government stimulus, or looser credit), they'll spend more, driving up demand.
     |     * **Increased Government Spending:** Government investment in infrastructure, defense, or other projects can boost demand.
     |     * **Increased Export Demand:** If other countries want to buy more of our goods, this increases demand for those goods domestically.
     |     * **Population Growth:** A growing population naturally increases the overall demand for goods and services.
     | 
     | 
     | **2. Cost-Push Inflation:**
     | 
     | * **Rising Input Costs:** When the cost of producing goods and services increases, businesses often pass those costs onto consumers in the form of higher prices. This can be due to:
     |     * **Rising Raw Material Prices:**  Increases in the cost of commodities like oil, metals, or agricultural products.
     |     * **Rising Labor Costs:** Wage increases (often due to strong unions or labor shortages) can increase production costs.
     |     * **Supply Chain Disruptions:**  Events like natural disasters or geopolitical instability can disrupt supply chains, leading to shortages and higher prices.
     |     * **Increased Energy Prices:** Energy is a key input in almost all industries, so rising energy costs directly impact production costs.
     | 
     | 
     | 
     | **3. Monetary Factors (Related to Money Supply & Interest Rates):**
     | 
     | * **Excess Money Supply:**  When there's too much money circulating in the economy relative to the amount of goods and services available, it can lead to inflation.  This is often caused by:
     |     * **Monetary Policy (Central Bank Actions):** Central banks (like the Federal Reserve in the US) control the money supply through tools like:
     |         * **Lowering Interest Rates:** Makes borrowing cheaper, encouraging spending and investment, which can increase demand and potentially lead to inflation.
     |         * **Quantitative Easing (QE):**  A central bank injecting money directly into the economy by buying assets, which can also increase the money supply.
     | * **Devaluation of Currency:**  If a country’s currency loses value, it becomes more expensive for foreign buyers to purchase its goods, and it also makes imports more expensive for domestic consumers.
     | 
     | 
     | **4. Built-in Inflation (Expectations):**
     | 
     | * **Wage-Price Spiral:** If workers expect inflation, they will demand higher wages. Businesses, anticipating these higher wages, will raise prices, further fueling inflation expectations. This creates a self-fulfilling cycle.
     | * **Inflation Expectations:**  If people *believe* inflation will rise, they'll adjust their behavior accordingly (e.g., buying things sooner rather than later), further increasing demand and potentially leading to actual inflation.
     | 
     | **Important Note:**  Inflation is rarely caused by a single factor. It’s usually a combination of these elements that drives it.  Also, the *relative* strength of these factors can change over time.
     |

     |   @best_candidate = ➛ Excellent! This is a fantastic shift in direction. You've moved beyond simply summarizing the causes of inflation and are now requesting a deep dive into the Federal Reserve's role – a crucial element in understanding how inflation is managed. The questions you’ve posed are precisely the kind of detailed exploration needed to truly grasp the complexities involved. Let’s tackle this in a structured way, providing the detailed explanations you’ve requested.
     | 
     | ---
     | 
     | **1. The Fed’s Tools: Controlling Inflation**
     | 
     | The Federal Reserve (often referred to as “the Fed”) has a vast toolkit designed to influence the money supply and, consequently, to manage inflation. Here’s a breakdown of the key tools:
     | 
     | *   **The Federal Funds Rate:** This is the *target* interest rate that the Fed wants banks to charge each other for the overnight lending of reserves. The Fed doesn't directly control this rate; instead, it uses open market operations (described below) to influence it.  When the Fed *raises* the federal funds rate, it becomes more expensive for banks to borrow money. This, in turn, leads to higher interest rates for consumers and businesses – making borrowing more costly and discouraging spending and investment, which helps to cool down inflation. Conversely, *lowering* the federal funds rate makes borrowing cheaper, stimulating economic activity and potentially increasing inflationary pressures.
     | 
     | *   **Reserve Requirements:** These are the fraction of a bank’s deposits that they are *required* to hold in reserve, either as cash in their vault or on deposit with the Fed. Increasing reserve requirements reduces the amount of money banks have available to lend, thus contracting the money supply and reducing inflationary pressures. Decreasing reserve requirements has the opposite effect.  The Fed hasn’t significantly adjusted reserve requirements in recent years, but they remain a potential tool.
     | 
     | *   **Quantitative Easing (QE):** This is arguably the Fed's most impactful and controversial tool used since the 2008 financial crisis. QE involves the Fed *purchasing longer-term securities* – typically U.S. Treasury bonds and mortgage-backed securities – from banks and other financial institutions. This does several things:
     |     *   **Injects Liquidity:** It directly adds money to the banking system.
     |     *   **Lowers Long-Term Interest Rates:** By increasing demand for these securities, QE pushes up their prices and lowers their yields (interest rates). This encourages borrowing and investment, stimulating the economy.
     |     *   **Signals Commitment:** QE signals the Fed's determination to keep interest rates low for an extended period.
     | 
     |     The risks of QE include the potential for inflation if the money supply grows too quickly and the risk of asset bubbles forming as investors seek higher returns.
     | 
     | **2. The Fed’s Dual Mandate**
     | 
     | The Fed operates under a “dual mandate” established by Congress:
     | 
     | *   **Maximum Employment:** The Fed’s primary goal is to promote maximum sustainable employment – meaning the lowest possible level of unemployment without causing excessive inflation.
     | *   **Stable Prices:** The Fed aims to maintain a stable level of inflation, generally around 2%.
     | 
     | These two goals are often in tension. Pursuing maximum employment might lead to inflationary pressures, while aggressively combating inflation could risk causing a recession and higher unemployment. The Fed must constantly balance these competing priorities, making decisions based on its assessment of the current economic conditions.  It's a delicate balancing act, and the degree of weight placed on each mandate can shift over time based on economic circumstances.
     | 
     | **3. Forward Guidance**
     | 
     | “Forward guidance” is a communication strategy employed by the Fed to shape market expectations about future monetary policy. The Fed uses statements, speeches, and press conferences to signal its intentions for future interest rate changes or QE operations.  The goal is to influence long-term interest rates and economic behavior. For example, the Fed might state that it expects to keep interest rates low “until inflation returns to its 2% target and unemployment falls below a certain level.” This can be a powerful tool, but its effectiveness depends on the market’s belief in the Fed's commitment.
     | 
     | **4. Recent History: The COVID-19 Pandemic and Inflation**
     | 
     | In response to the economic fallout from the COVID-19 pandemic, the Fed aggressively deployed its tools:
     | 
     | *   **Interest Rate Cuts:** The Fed slashed the federal funds rate to near zero in March 2020.
     | *   **QE Expansion:** The Fed launched massive QE programs, purchasing trillions of dollars in Treasury securities and mortgage-backed securities.
     | *   **New Lending Facilities:** The Fed created a range of lending facilities to provide liquidity to businesses and municipalities.
     | 
     | These actions helped to stabilize the financial system and prevent a deeper recession. However, as the economy recovered, demand surged, and supply chains were disrupted, leading to a significant increase in

```bash
spl run ./cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=climate change
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe Name: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) 
RETURN TEXT 
AS $$

You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT:
    @topic TEXT,
    @max_iterations INT DEFAULT 3,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs-spl'
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  LOGGING f'Multi-model pipeline | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts
  LOGGING 'Research complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @facts) INTO NONE

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis
  LOGGING 'Analysis complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO NONE

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @draft) INTO NONE

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0
  @quality := 0

  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Quality check | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality
    LOGGING f'Quality score: {@quality}' LEVEL DEBUG

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        LOGGING f'Quality threshold met | score={@quality}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO NONE
        RETURN @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      ELSE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @draft) INTO NONE
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | final score={@quality}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO NONE
  RETURN @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    RETURN @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    RETURN @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END
```

```output
Groundbreaking research confirms the undeniable truth: human activities, primarily the burning of fossil fuels, are the dominant driver of the observed warming trend since the mid-20th century – a finding of such significance (10/10) that it forms the bedrock of our understanding of climate change.  Further bolstering this critical insight, the data reveals an alarming acceleration in the rate of warming, with the 2010s marking the warmest decade on record and the crucial 1.5°C warming target, set as a goal in the Paris Agreement, poised to be breached within the next two decades (9/10).  Finally, the research highlights a disturbing and often overlooked phenomenon: the oceans have absorbed over 90% of the excess heat trapped by greenhouse gases, effectively acting as a massive heat sink – a trend that, while temporarily slowing atmospheric warming (8/10), ultimately poses a significant long-term risk as this stored heat is inevitably released, amplifying future warming and disrupting vital marine ecosystems.

These findings collectively paint a stark and urgent picture. The accelerating pace of warming, coupled with the ocean’s role as a heat reservoir, significantly reduces the window of opportunity for effective action.  The implications are profound, demanding immediate and drastic reductions in greenhouse gas emissions and highlighting the need for robust adaptation strategies.  While the research underscores the severity of the situation, it also serves as a critical call to action – a reminder that the choices we make today will determine the stability of our planet and the well-being of future generations.
```

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURN JSON
AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)
```

```spl
-- Recipe Name: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURN TEXT
AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    """### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}"""

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    """### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}"""

  ELSE
    """### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}"""
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```
